[0:0:66,13] 2023-24 STATE BUDGET — LJE   172 

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:207,32] 0840   State Controller - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] The Personnel and Payroll Services Division (PPSD) processes payroll and leave accounting for all state civil service and  

[3:1:66,78] exempt employees, state court, and California State University employees. PPSD provides information required to manage the  

[3:2:66,88] personnel resources of the state and to properly account for salary and wage expenditures, provides data to the retirement  

[3:3:66,98] systems necessary for calculation of employee retirement benefits, and manages the state’s automated travel expense  

[3:4:66,108] reimbursement system.  

[4:0:66,127] Cal Employee Connect (CEC) and ConnectHR are SCO’s secure web-based customer service portals for state employees and  

[4:1:66,137] human resources staff, respectively. They were developed as interim solutions to provide some of the enhancements the  

[4:2:66,147] California State Payroll System Project (CSPS) will deliver when completed, to support a hybrid remote workforce and make  

[4:3:66,156] personnel and payroll transactions more efficient and paperless.  

[5:0:66,174] The CSPS Project was initiated to modernize the statewide human resource management, travel and business expense, and  

[5:1:66,183] payroll   systems as part of a fully integrated solution. The system should enable the state to improve management processes  

[5:2:66,193] and fulfill its payroll, reporting, and deduction processing obligations accurately and on time.  

[5:3:66,211] 0500400 - UNCLAIMED PROPERTY  

[6:0:66,230] The Unclaimed Property Division administers the Unclaimed Property Law by providing one central source for rightful owners to  

[6:1:66,240] claim their property; notifying owners of their unclaimed property before it is transferred to the state; receiving unclaimed  

[6:2:66,250] property from banks, savings and loans, insurance companies, and other businesses; and providing outreach and education for  

[6:3:66,259] businesses holding unclaimed property. 

[6:4:66,277] 0500500 - DISBURSEMENTS  

[7:0:66,296] The Disbursements Bureau produces and distributes all payments of the state's obligations after auditing and clearance  

[7:1:66,306] through control accounts.   Examples of some of the state's payments are personal income tax refunds, payroll, vendor  

[7:2:66,316] payments,   and retirement payments. Disbursements also provides post-issuance services including disposition of undeliverable  

[7:3:66,326] warrants; lost warrant duplication; and maintenance and production of paid warrant images.  

[7:4:66,343] 0500900 - DEPARTMENTAL ADMINISTRATION  

[8:0:66,362] The Executive Office and Administration   functions include Budgets, Business Services, Contracts and Procurement,  

[8:1:66,372] Departmental Accounting, and Human Resources all of which provide executive direction and support services to all   divisions  

[8:2:66,382] within SCO.  

[9:0:66,399] The Information Systems Division (ISD)   develops, maintains, and operates all of the department's mainframe, client-server, and  

[9:1:66,409] web-based systems; and oversees the planning, procurement, use, and maintenance of information technology hardware and  

[9:2:66,419] software. Within ISD, the Information Security Office evaluates security compliance, internal control management, and security  

[9:3:66,429] governance.

[7:0:216,354] 0845   Department of Insurance  

[8:0:66,375] California's elected Insurance Commissioner regulates the second largest insurance market in the world with insurance  

[8:1:66,385] companies collecting more than $409 billion in premiums annually in California while protecting consumers and ensuring the  

[8:2:66,395] health and sustainability of the insurance marketplace. The vision of the California Department of Insurance (CDI) is "Insurance  

[8:3:66,405] Protection for All Californians." CDI accomplishes this by enforcing insurance laws and regulations, assisting consumers in their  

[8:4:66,414] dealings with insurance companies, and enhancing business processes to better address emerging and future challenges as  

[8:5:66,424] well as improving services for insurance producers, consumers, and businesses.  

[9:0:66,441] CDI licenses and regulates insurance companies, and individuals in California. Currently, CDI oversees and licenses more than  

[9:1:66,451] 1,400 insurance companies and more than 485,000 individuals and business entities as insurance agents, brokers, adjusters,  

[9:2:66,461] and bail agents and annually issues approximately 280,000 licenses (new and renewals). Additionally, CDI receives and  

[9:3:66,471] investigates approximately 210,000 consumer inquiries and complaints annually, performs hundreds of ongoing risk-focused  

[9:4:66,480] financial surveillances and market conduct examinations of insurance companies, receives more than 20,000 suspected  

[9:5:66,490] fraudulent claim referrals and investigates approximately 1,300 of those referrals, receives and reviews approximately 6,300  

[9:6:66,500] rate filing applications annually, and works in conjunction with local, state, and federal law enforcement agencies to investigate  

[9:7:66,510] and prosecute fraudulent insurance practices.

[15:0:66,538] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[16:0:66,557] 0520 - REGULATION OF INSURANCE COMPANIES AND INSURANCE PRODUCERS  

[17:0:66,575] The objectives of this program are to: (1) oversee the financial solvency of insurance companies to make sure they can provide  

[17:1:66,584] the benefits and protections promised to California policyholders; (2) review complex, principles-based reserving methods and  

[17:2:66,594] underlying assumptions to ensure transparency and consistency in models; (3) prevent unlawful or unfair practices by  

[17:3:66,604] insurance companies and insurance producers as defined by the Insurance Code; (4) ensure that property and casualty  

[17:4:66,614] insurance rates are not excessive, inadequate, unfairly discriminatory, or otherwise in violation of the California Insurance Code  

[17:5:66,623] and the California Code of Regulations; (5) assess and implement strategies to reduce climate risk impacts in the insurance  

[17:6:66,633] sector; (6) review health insurance rates filed with CDI to determine whether they are reasonable and work with insurance  

[17:7:66,643] companies to get unreasonable rates lowered; (7) review long term care insurance rates to ensure requested rate increases  

[17:8:66,653] are actuarially justified and not excessive; and (8) perform market conduct reviews to ensure compliance; and (9) ensure that  

[17:9:66,662] applicants for insurance licenses, and holders of insurance licenses, satisfy and maintain the qualifications for licensure.  

[17:10:66,672] Additionally, CDI administers the estates of insolvent and delinquent insurance companies through the Conservation and  

[17:11:66,682] Liquidation Office.  

[17:12:66,699] 0525 - CONSUMER PROTECTION  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:410,13] 2023-24 STATE BUDGET — LJE   181 

[2:0:181,32] 0845   Department of Insurance - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] The objectives of this program are to: (1) provide direct service to California consumers by protecting insurance policyholders  

[3:1:66,78] and other parties involved in insurance transactions against unfair or illegal practices with respect to claims handling, rating, or  

[3:2:66,88] underwriting by insurers and producers; (2) protect applicants and policyholders from discriminatory, unlawful, or fraudulent  

[3:3:66,98] practices and from incompetence relating to the sale of insurance; (3) oversee programs that benefit California's underserved  

[3:4:66,108] and historically disadvantaged communities; and (4) assist wildfire survivors, local governments, small businesses, community  

[3:5:66,117] service organizations, and neighborhood associations by providing access to CDI’s services through electronic and printed  

[3:6:66,127] informational guides, workshops, seminars, roundtables, and town halls.  

[3:7:66,144] 0530 - FRAUD CONTROL  

[4:0:66,162] The objective of this program is to protect the public from economic loss by actively investigating, arresting and referring for  

[4:1:66,171] prosecution those who commit insurance fraud and other violations of the law. The program is primarily staffed by sworn peace  

[4:2:66,181] officers who conduct criminal investigations of insurance fraud and related criminal cases. The program administers the  

[4:3:66,191] Insurance Frauds Prevention Act (IFPA), which authorizes CDI to conduct criminal insurance fraud investigations. In addition,  

[4:4:66,201] the IFPA authorizes the Commissioner to initiate or intervene in whistle-blower "qui tam" actions seeking civil penalties against  

[4:5:66,211] perpetrators of insurance fraud.  

[4:6:66,228] 0535 - GENERAL FUND TAX COLLECTION AND COMPLIANCE  

[5:0:66,245] This program performs tax collection, ensures the compliance of insurance companies and surplus line brokers with the laws  

[5:1:66,255] contained in the Insurance Code and the Revenue and Taxation Code, and works with the California Department of Tax and  

[5:2:66,265] Fee Administration and the State Controller's Office on various refund, assessment, and accounting matters relative to the

[6:0:183,218] 0850   California State Lottery Commission  

[6:1:66,239] In 1984, Proposition 37 amended the California Constitution to authorize the establishment of a statewide lottery. As an  

[6:2:66,249] initiative statute, the California State Lottery Act (Act) of 1984 created the California State Lottery Commission and gave it  

[6:3:66,259] broad powers to oversee the operations of a statewide lottery. The purpose of the Act was to provide supplemental monies to  

[6:4:66,268] benefit public education. The Lottery is overseen by a five-person Commission appointed by the Governor and confirmed by  

[6:5:66,278] the State Senate.  

[7:0:66,296] The Act initially required that 50 percent of total annual revenues be returned to the public in the form of prizes and at least 34  

[7:1:66,305] percent of total revenues be allocated to the benefit of public education. No more than 16 percent of total revenues were to be  

[7:2:66,315] used for administrative costs.  

[8:0:66,332] In 2010, the Act was changed to allow the Lottery flexibility to pay out more money in prizes and reduce the administrative cost  

[8:1:66,342] limit to 13 percent of total revenues. Along with that flexibility, the new law requires the Lottery to meet minimum levels of  

[8:2:66,352] contribution to public education. Revenues to education are placed in a special fund, known as the California State Lottery  

[8:3:66,362] Education Fund, which holds revenues until they are allocated on a per capita basis, using prior year certified Average Daily  

[8:4:66,371] Attendance data, to the following categories: K-12 education, Community Colleges, the California State University, the  

[8:5:66,381] University of California, and other educational entities, including the California Schools for the Deaf and Blind.  

[8:6:66,398] In the 38 years since sales began in October 1985 through June 30, 2023, the California State Lottery has raised $43.7 billion  

[8:7:66,408] for public education, including approximately $2.0 billion in 2022-23.  

[9:0:66,426] Because of the inherently variable nature of lottery ticket sales, revenue estimates for 2022-23 and 2023-24 cannot be made  

[9:1:66,435] with certainty.  

[10:0:66,462] LEGAL CITATIONS AND AUTHORITY  

[10:1:66,481] DEPARTMENT AUTHORITY  

[11:0:66,500] Government Code, Title 2, Division 1, Chapter 12.5 (Sections 8880-8880.72).  

[12:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:169,65] 0855   California Gambling Control Commission  

[2:0:66,86] The mission of the California Gambling Control Commission (Commission) is to protect the public by ensuring integrity and  

[2:1:66,96] justice in California's controlled gambling industry through effective regulations and fair application of the law. The Commission  

[2:2:66,106] has jurisdiction over gambling establishments (cardrooms), third-party providers of proposition player services, and certain  

[2:3:66,115] aspects of Tribal casinos, pursuant to its authority under state law and Tribal-State Gaming Compacts (Compacts). The  

[2:4:66,125] Commission also has jurisdiction over gaming policies, regulations, criteria, and standards.  

[3:0:66,142] There are 86 licensed cardrooms in California over which the Commission has broad regulatory and adjudicatory authority. This  

[3:1:66,152] authority extends to the operation, concentration, and supervision of the cardrooms and all persons and things related to each  

[3:2:66,162] licensed establishment.  

[4:0:66,179] The Commission has fiduciary, regulatory, and administrative responsibilities related to Tribal gaming that include: (1)  

[4:1:66,189] distributing specified Tribal gaming revenues to Non-Compact, Non-Gaming and Limited-Gaming Tribes; (2) making suitability  

[4:2:66,199] determinations for key employees, gaming resource suppliers, and financial sources; and (3) serving as the administrator of the  

[4:3:66,209] Indian Gaming Revenue Sharing Trust Fund, Indian Gaming Special Distribution Fund, and the Tribal Nation Grant Fund.

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE

[3:0:66,68] This program ensures that taxable properties are enrolled and assessed by the 58 county assessors in full conformity with the  

[3:1:66,78] law. The BOE is also charged with providing consultation and services to the county assessors and their staff, establishing  

[3:2:66,88] standards, providing training for assessors and assessment appeals boards, evaluating the effectiveness of each county  

[3:3:66,98] assessor's administration, co-administering the welfare exemption, and administering the Legal Entity Ownership Program.  

[3:4:66,115] 0570050 - STATE-ASSESSED PROPERTY PROGRAM  

[4:0:66,135] This program   values and assesses   inter-county pipelines and properties owned or used by railroads, regulated telephone  

[4:1:66,144] companies, companies transmitting or selling gas or electricity, and private railroad car companies. Local jurisdictions use the  

[4:2:66,154] established values for the levy and collection of local property taxes. In addition, the BOE collects the Private Railroad Car Tax  

[4:3:66,164] that is deposited in the state General Fund.  

[4:4:66,181] 0570150 - ALCOHOLIC BEVERAGE TAX PROGRAM  

[5:0:66,201] This program collects the excise tax imposed on the sale, distribution, or importation of alcoholic beverages in California. The  

[5:1:66,211] BOE contracts with the CDTFA to administer this program.  

[5:2:66,228] 0570575 - INSURANCE TAX PROGRAM  

[6:0:66,247] This program provides revenue to the Insurance Tax Fund. Revenue is generated from taxes assessed on insurance  

[6:1:66,257] premiums, underwriting profits from ocean marine insurance, and retaliatory assessments levied on   out-of-state insurers. The  

[6:2:66,267] taxes collected are used to pay refunds or transferred to the General Fund. The BOE contracts with the CDTFA to administer  

[6:3:66,277] this program.

[11:0:226,561] 0870   Office of Tax Appeals  

[12:0:66,582] The Office of Tax Appeals (OTA) was established effective January 1, 2018, by Chapter 16, Statutes of 2017 (AB 102). The  

[12:1:66,591] OTA performs those tax appeal duties formerly assigned by statute to the State Board of Equalization, including personal  

[12:2:66,601] income tax appeals, franchise tax appeals, and sales and use tax appeals. The OTA's mission is to ensure tax appeals are  

[12:3:66,611] performed in a fair, transparent, consistent, equitable, and impartial manner.  

[13:0:66,630] The OTA has hearing offices in Sacramento, Fresno, and Los Angeles.

[11:0:236,600] 0890   Secretary of State  

[12:0:66,620] The Secretary of State, a constitutionally established office, is the chief elections officer of the state and is responsible for the  

[12:1:66,630] administration and enforcement of election laws. The Office also is responsible for administering and enforcing laws pertaining  

[12:2:66,640] to filing documents associated with corporations, limited liability companies, partnerships, limited partnerships, unincorporated  

[12:3:66,650] associations and pertaining to filing bonds and perfecting security agreements. In addition, the Office is responsible for  

[12:4:66,660] commissioning notaries public, enforcing the notary laws, and in conjunction with being the home of the State Archives,  

[12:5:66,669] administering a state records management program and preserving documents and records having historical significance. The  

[12:6:66,679] Secretary of State is the filing officer for lobbying and campaign registration and disclosure documents filed under the Political  

[12:7:66,689] Reform Act. The Secretary of State also operates the Safe at Home program, maintains the Domestic Partners and Advance  

[12:8:66,699] Health Care Directives registries, provides support functions for the Voting Modernization Board, and is home to the California  

[12:9:66,708] Museum.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[4:0:66,87] 0700 - FILINGS AND REGISTRATIONS  

[5:0:66,107] The Business Programs Division is comprised of three primary areas: Business Entities, Uniform Commercial Code, and  

[5:1:66,117] Notary Public and Special Filings.  

[6:0:66,136] Business Entities examines, files, and maintains formation documents for new California business entities and qualifies out-of-

[6:1:66,146] state and international entities to conduct business in California; and also examines, files and maintains changes to the records  

[6:2:66,156] of domestic or qualified foreign business entities to ensure the business entities are properly formed, merged, converted,  

[6:3:66,166] amended, and dissolved in compliance with California law. Statements of Information containing key officers and contact  

[6:4:66,175] information are also processed and maintained for public and governmental use.  

[7:0:66,195] Uniform Commercial Code files certain financing statements allowing lending institutions to protect their financial interest in  

[7:1:66,205] personal property and established priority in case of debtor default or bankruptcy. Notices of judgment liens, attachment liens,  

[7:2:66,215] agricultural liens, and equipment liens are also filed in Uniform Commercial Code, as well as notices of various tax liens  

[7:3:66,224] submitted by state and federal taxing agencies such as the Franchise Tax Board and the Internal Revenue Service.  

[8:0:66,244] Notary Public and Special Filings appoints and commissions qualified notaries public for four- year terms, reviews and  

[9:0:66,254] approves notary public education courses, and certifies to the authenticity of signatures of notaries public and public officials on  

[9:1:66,263] documents for use in foreign countries. Special Filings include trademarks and service marks as well as surety bonds that  

[9:2:66,273] certain business entities are required to file, notices of joint power agreements, city and county charters, and claims for  

[9:3:66,283] successor-in-interest. This area also has disciplinary functions with regard to notaries public and immigration consultants.  

[10:0:66,303] The Advance Health Care Directive (AHCD) Registry maintains AHCDs, which allow a person to indicate his/her medical  

[10:1:66,312] treatment preferences if he/she cannot speak or make decisions for him/herself. The AHCD also may designate someone else  

[10:2:66,322] to make decisions regarding medical treatment. AHCDs and related information can be provided upon request to the  

[10:3:66,332] registrant's health care provider, public guardian, or legal representative.  

[11:0:66,351] The Domestic Partners Registry registers same-sex couples regardless of the age of the partners, and opposite-sex couples in  

[11:1:66,361] which one partner is at least 62 years old, as domestic partners in California.  

[12:0:66,381] The Victims of Corporate Fraud Compensation Fund provides limited restitution to victims of corporate fraud who are unable to  

[12:1:66,390] collect through other means on a judgment against a corporation for corporate fraud.  

[13:0:66,408] Safe at Home is the State's confidential address program designed to assist: (1) victims and survivors of domestic violence and  

[13:1:66,418] human trafficking, sexual assault, stalking, and elder/dependent adult abuse; (2) reproductive health care employees, patients,  

[13:2:66,427] and volunteers who are in fear for their safety because of their affiliation with a health care facility; (3) public entity employees  

[13:3:66,437] and contractors who are in fear for their safety because of their work for a public entity; and (4) their household members and  

[13:4:66,447] minor children. Participants in the Safe at Home program are provided with a post office box to use as a substitute mailing  

[13:5:66,457] address, thereby allowing them to keep their actual residential address confidential. Once enrolled into the program, Safe at  

[13:6:66,466] Home   provides a mail forwarding service and assists participants who qualify with additional confidential services offered by  

[13:7:66,476] various state and local agencies.  

[14:0:66,493] 0705 - ELECTIONS  

[15:0:66,513] The Secretary of State serves as California’s chief elections officer and has broad responsibility to administer and oversee  

[15:1:66,523] federal and state elections held within California. The Elections Program maintains the official statewide database of registered  

[15:2:66,533] voters, tracks and certifies ballot measures, certifies candidates for placement on the ballot, and certifies official election  

[15:3:66,542] results. In addition, the program oversees the certification and modernization of voting equipment and the enhancement of  

[15:4:66,552] election processes. The program works to increase accessibility to voters, provides resources to educate voters on the  

[15:5:66,562] electoral system, and investigates election related criminal violations as provided by the California Elections Code.  

[16:0:66,581] This program includes the campaign and lobbying registration and disclosure provisions of the Political Reform Act of 1974.  

[16:1:66,591] Under this law, the Office registers all levels of campaign committees, and reviews for compliance with statutory requirements  

[16:2:66,601] all state-level candidate and campaign recipient committees, major donors, slate mailer, political party, and independent  

[16:3:66,611] expenditure committees. The program registers lobbyists (including placement agents), lobbying firms and lobbyist employers,  

[16:4:66,621] reviews for compliance with statutory requirements the periodic financial disclosure reports filed by lobbying entities, and  

[16:5:66,630] publishes a Lobbying Directory on the Internet. This program also supports the electronic filing requirements of the Political  

[16:6:66,640] Reform Act and posts relevant registration and disclosure information to the Office's web site so the public can track where  

[16:7:66,650] campaign money is raised and spent and what lobbying activity is occurring in state government. A searchable database of  

[16:8:66,660] campaign and lobbying financial activity is also provided through the web site.  

[17:0:66,677] 0710 - ARCHIVES  

[18:0:66,696] The California State Archives establishes and administers a records management program that applies efficient and  

[18:1:66,706] economical management methods to the creation, utilization, maintenance, retention, preservation, and disposal of state  

[18:2:66,716] records and acquires, indexes, preserves, and provides reference access to irreplaceable historic materials from the three  

[19:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,68] branches of state government. The Archives acquires physical and electronic records with legal, administrative, or evidentiary  

[3:1:66,78] value, or that have historical significance. Materials are transferred to the Archives for permanent preservation.  

[3:2:66,96] 9900 - ADMINISTRATION  

[4:0:66,115] This program develops and manages overall policy. It serves as the liaison to other state agencies, the Legislature, the federal  

[4:1:66,125] government, and other states' Secretaries of State. This program responds to constituent correspondence and requests from  

[4:2:66,135] the public and advocates in the Legislature, administers the Secretary's role as a member of the National Association of  

[4:3:66,144] Secretaries of State, in the management of task forces and advisory committees, in the implementation of new federal and  

[4:4:66,154] state statutory requirements, and coordinates and disseminates information about the Office and its initiatives to the public.  

[4:5:66,164] This program also monitors elections cybersecurity incidents and promotes transparency in alerting the public regarding false  

[4:6:66,174] election websites and information as well as providing oversight on cybersecurity risk management.   It also provides the Office  

[4:7:66,183] with the necessary personnel, general administrative, budgeting, fiscal services, and information technology expertise to plan,  

[4:8:66,193] develop, implement, and operate innovative and effective business and information systems solutions.

[10:0:66,483] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[10:1:66,501] 0740010 - INVESTMENT SERVICES  

[11:0:66,521] The Investment Division is responsible for investment of state monies from the date of receipt through the date of redemption.  

[11:1:66,531] During the 2021-22 fiscal year, this Division handled 12,002 security investment transactions totaling $589.2 billion. The Pooled  

[11:2:66,540] Money Investment Board (PMIB) program accounted for 11,072 of these transactions totaling $552.9 billion; time deposits  

[11:3:66,550] accounted for 759 transactions totaling $23.3 billion. The remaining $13.0 billion is invested on behalf of the state's special  

[11:4:66,560] funds, such as those associated with the California Housing Finance Agency, the Department of Fish and Wildlife, the state's  

[11:5:66,570] retirement system, etc. The Division also administers the Local Agency Investment Fund (LAIF), a voluntary investment  

[11:6:66,580] program created to offer California local agencies greater access to the financial markets through the PMIB program. In the  

[11:7:66,589] 2021-22 fiscal year, 2,387 local agencies participated in LAIF, with deposits averaging $36.0 billion for the fiscal year.  

[11:8:66,607] 0740019 - CENTRALIZED TREASURY AND SECURITIES MANAGEMENT  

[12:0:66,626] In 1949, the California Legislature amended Government Code Section 16305 to create the Centralized Treasury System  

[12:1:66,636] (CTS) thereby requiring agencies of the State to deposit their money in trust with the Treasurer. The legislation also requires  

[12:2:66,646] the Treasurer to safeguard the money and make safe and prudent investments. In 1955, the Legislature created the PMIB,  

[12:3:66,655] giving the Board the responsibility to designate the amount of money available for the investment in securities, bank deposits,  

[12:4:66,665] and loans to the General Fund.  

[13:0:66,685] The Centralized Treasury and Securities Management Division (CTSMD) oversees all banking aspects of the CTS. The goal of  

[13:1:66,695] the CTSMD is to maximize the earning of interest consistent with safe and prudent treasury management, and to ensure that  

[13:2:66,704] the depository banks provide the state with proper and adequate security for the deposit of state monies. The Treasurer  

[13:3:66,714] maintains demand bank accounts with eight banks for the purpose of providing necessary statewide depository coverage for  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 213 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:410,13] 2023-24 STATE BUDGET — LJE   211 

[2:0:208,32] 0950   State Treasurer - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] the remittance of funds collected by the various state agencies.  

[4:0:66,88] The CTSMD 1) monitors the cash flow of all state funds, forecasts cash balances, revenue, expenditures, and the amounts  

[4:1:66,98] available for daily investments, 2) ensures accurate and timely agency deposits, 3) administers and executes the wire transfer  

[4:2:66,108] of funds, 4) reconciles state accounts with depository banks and redeems all state items submitted by presenting banks for  

[4:3:66,117] payments, 5) executes the clearance and income collection for state investments (excluding PERS and STRS) and securities  

[4:4:66,127] pledged to the state, 6) fulfills the clearance and settlement of securities pledged to the state as collateral for the time, demand,  

[4:5:66,137] and other state agency programs, and 7) is responsible for the safekeeping of securities and other personal property owned by  

[4:6:66,147] or pledged to the state.  

[4:7:66,164] 0740028 - PUBLIC FINANCE  

[5:0:66,183] The Public Finance Division is responsible for selling State of California general obligation bonds, commercial paper notes,  

[5:1:66,193] revenue anticipation notes, lease revenue bonds, revenue bonds, and any other indebtedness for all state agencies. The  

[5:2:66,203] Division provides debt administration services for most state bonds and is responsible for disseminating information to  

[5:3:66,213] bondholders through the Investor Relations Program.  

[6:0:66,232] The Division also assures compliance with federal tax laws applicable to certain state debt so there is no adverse  

[6:1:66,242] consequences to bondholders or increased borrowing costs to the state. The post-issuance tax compliance   includes monitoring  

[6:2:66,252] the use of bond proceeds, managing rebate and yield restriction compliance, coordinating necessary financial disclosure, and  

[6:3:66,262] ensuring proper record keeping.

[7:0:134,317] 0956   California Debt and Investment Advisory Commission  

[7:1:66,338] The mission of the California Debt and Investment Advisory Commission is to support and improve the practice of public  

[7:2:66,347] finance in California by providing responsive and reliable information, analysis, and training. The Commission assists California  

[7:3:66,357] state and local governments to monitor, issue, and manage public debt and to safely and prudently invest public funds.  

[8:0:66,377] The Commission consists of nine members including the State Treasurer, who serves as chairperson; the Governor or, upon  

[8:1:66,386] his designation, the Director of Finance; the State Controller; two local government finance officers appointed by the State  

[8:2:66,396] Treasurer; two members of the Assembly appointed by the Speaker of the Assembly; and two members of the Senate  

[8:3:66,406] appointed by the Senate Committee on Rules.

[6:0:66,248] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:1:66,267] 0800 - CALIFORNIA DEBT AND INVESTMENT ADVISORY COMMISSION  

[7:0:66,286] The Commission's primary objective is to enhance the knowledge of public officials with respect to the administration and  

[7:1:66,296] issuance of public debt and the investment of public funds. The Commission achieves this objective by performing functions in  

[7:2:66,306] three principal areas, including:  

[8:0:65,323] • Data Collection and Analysis - Serves as a clearinghouse of debt issued by California public entities and nonprofit student  

[8:1:76,333] loan corporations.  

[9:0:65,349] • Continuing Education - Provides educational seminars, workshops, and conferences to public officials on municipal debt and  

[9:1:76,359] public fund investment topics. In addition, the Commission provides technical assistance to public officials and taxpayers  

[9:2:76,368] through direct interaction and public forums to disseminate relevant information.  

[9:3:65,384] • Policy Research - Undertakes original research on the issuance and administration of public debt and on the investment of  

[9:4:76,394] public funds. Research results are published in the form of reports, issue briefs, or articles and are disseminated in printed  

[9:5:76,404] and electronic forms.

[10:0:165,608] 0959   California Debt Limit Allocation Committee  

[11:0:66,629] The California Debt Limit Allocation Committee's mission is to allocate tax-exempt, private activity bond authority for the State  

[11:1:66,639] of California. Private activity bonds may be used by the private sector for projects and programs that provide a public benefit,  

[11:2:66,649] such as the creation of affordable housing.  

[12:0:66,666] The federal government limits the total amount of tax-exempt private activity bond authority that can be issued on an annual  

[12:1:66,676] basis. The bond authority limit (debt limit) is calculated by multiplying the state population by $110 in 2022; a factor determined  

[12:2:66,686] by the IRS each year. California's limit totaled over $4.3 billion in 2022.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:130,32] 0959   California Debt Limit Allocation Committee - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] The Committee's allocation of tax-exempt bond authority results in the issuance of bonds by cities, counties, joint powers  

[3:1:66,78] authorities and state agencies. The bonds are purchased and used by the private sector and are not an obligation of the state  

[3:2:66,88] or federal government.  

[4:0:66,105] The Committee is funded on a fee-supported basis and is comprised of six members, including three voting members and three  

[4:1:66,115] advisors. The voting members are the State Treasurer (Chairperson of the Committee), the Governor, and the State Controller  

[4:2:66,125] or their designee. The non-voting (advisory) members are the Director of Housing and Community Development, the Executive  

[4:3:66,135] Director of the California Housing Finance Agency, and a representative from local government selected by two voting  

[4:4:66,144] members.

[13:0:66,606] The Committee administers various programs that are funded through the allocation and issuance of tax-exempt private activity  

[13:1:66,616] bonds. Below is a summary of each program:  

[14:0:66,633] Qualified Residential Rental Project Program - Tax-exempt housing revenue bonds lower the cost to develop multifamily rental  

[14:1:66,643] housing, to acquire land and construct new housing, or purchase and rehabilitate existing housing. The developers produce  

[14:2:66,653] affordable rental housing for low-income households by reducing rental rates charged to these individuals and families. To  

[14:3:66,662] qualify, developers must elect either to reserve at least 20% of the units for households earning up to 50% AMI or reserve at  

[14:4:66,672] least 40% of the units for households earning up to 60% AMI. Projects that receive an award of bond authority can apply for  

[14:5:66,682] four-percent tax credits using the California Debt Limit Allocation Committee/California Tax Credit Allocation Committee Joint  

[14:6:66,692] Application.  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,68] Exempt Facility Program - Tax-exempt private activity bonds issued to finance any of the following facilities identified by the IRS  

[3:1:66,78] as an exempt facility, including but not limited to: solid waste disposal and waste recycling facilities, mass commuting facilities,  

[3:2:66,88] high-speed rail, energy and power generating facilities, and sustainable design facilities. Loan interest rate savings enable the  

[3:3:66,98] project owners to charge lower customer rates, while assisting communities they serve to meet mandated requirements to  

[3:4:66,108] protect and enhance the environment.  

[3:5:66,125] Single-Family Housing Program - Tax-exempt mortgage revenue bonds or mortgage credit certificates to assist first-time  

[3:6:66,135] homebuyers with purchasing homes. Homebuyers may purchase single-family homes, either freestanding or detached,  

[3:7:66,144] condominiums, or townhouses. Program participants must meet income limits and must purchase a home that falls within the  

[3:8:66,154] program's purchase price limitations.  

[3:9:66,171] Industrial Development Bond Project Program - Small-Issue industrial development bonds are tax-exempt private activity bonds  

[3:10:66,181] issued to assist manufacturing facilities finance capital expenditures. These bonds offer interest rate savings to small and  

[3:11:66,191] midsize manufacturers in contrast to conventional loans, and help manufacturers retain and create new jobs within their  

[3:12:66,201] communities.

[13:0:166,577] 0968   California Tax Credit Allocation Committee  

[14:0:66,598] The mission of the California Tax Credit Allocation Committee is to fairly allocate federal and state tax credits to create and  

[14:1:66,607] maintain safe, quality affordable rental housing for low-income households in California.  

[15:0:66,625] The Committee assists development of rental housing projects for low-income families and monitors these projects for years  

[15:1:66,634] after they are placed in service to ensure compliance with federal and state tax credit program requirements of the Internal  

[15:2:66,644] Revenue Service, State law and the Committee. Coordinating with state and local housing fund providers, and with private  

[15:3:66,654] investors, the Committee helps to create and maintain a supply of quality, affordable housing throughout California.  

[15:4:66,671] The Committee consists of seven members, including five voting members and two advisors. The voting members are the  

[15:5:66,681] State Treasurer (Chairperson of the Committee), the Governor, the State Controller, the Director of Housing and Community  

[15:6:66,691] Development, and the Executive Director of the California Housing Finance Agency or their designee. The non-voting  

[15:7:66,701] (advisory) members are two local government representatives. One local representative is associated with a city government  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[11:0:66,548] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[12:0:66,567] 0840 - CALIFORNIA TAX CREDIT ALLOCATION COMMITTEE  

[13:0:66,586] The Federal Program  

[14:0:66,603] Congress authorized the federal Low-Income Housing Tax Credit (LIHTC) program in 1986. It replaced traditional housing tax  

[14:1:66,613] incentives, such as accelerated depreciation, with a tax credit that enables developers of affordable rental housing to raise  

[14:2:66,623] project equity through the "sale" of tax benefits to investors. The federal per-capita tax credit ceiling is calculated annually and  

[14:3:66,633] indexed for inflation.  

[15:0:66,650] Two types of federal tax credits are available, generally referred to as 9-percent and 4-percent credits. These terms refer to the  

[15:1:66,660] percentage of a project's "qualified basis" an investor in LIHTC projects may deduct from his or her federal income tax liability  

[15:2:66,670] annually in each of ten years. A project's qualified basis is the portion of the total project cost used to calculate the amount of  

[15:3:66,679] the tax credit and typically excludes non-depreciable costs such as the purchase of land. The affordable housing project  

[15:4:66,689] sponsor uses or sells ten times the project's tax-credit allocation amount because investors can take the annual tax credit each  

[15:5:66,699] year for a ten-year period.  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 229 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:410,13] 2023-24 STATE BUDGET — LJE   227 

[2:0:131,32] 0968   California Tax Credit Allocation Committee - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] The federal per-capita tax credit ceiling for 9-percent credits is calculated annually and indexed for inflation. For 2022, each  

[3:1:66,78] state has an annual housing credit ceiling of $2.60 per state resident. Additionally, each state may qualify annually for a share  

[3:2:66,88] of unused credits in a national pool.  

[4:0:66,105] Developments financed with tax-exempt bond proceeds may receive the 4% federal tax credit. The sponsors of such projects  

[4:1:66,115] must apply to the Committee and must conform to applicable federal and state statutory and regulatory requirements, but there  

[4:2:66,125] is no annual cap on the amount of credit that may be awarded by the state to such developments.  

[4:3:66,142] Under federal law, credit projects must remain affordable for at least 30 years. However, California's program generally requires  

[4:4:66,152] maintaining affordability for 55 years. Land use agreements are recorded against each credit project to ensure compliance.  

[4:5:66,169] The State LIHTC Program  

[5:0:66,186] In 1987, recognizing the high cost of housing in California, the Legislature authorized the state's program to augment the  

[5:1:66,196] federal program. Housing projects receiving (or that have previously received) an allocation of federal tax credits may apply for  

[5:2:66,206] an allocation of state tax credits. Projects serving farmworkers may apply for state tax credits regardless of their federal tax  

[5:3:66,216] credit status.  

[6:0:66,233] The annual state credit ceiling is indexed for inflation, and for 2022, was $102 million. Investors take the state tax credit  

[6:1:66,243] annually over a four-year period in contrast to the ten-year federal period.  

[6:2:66,260] Chapter 159, Statutes of 2019, authorized the California Tax Credit Allocation Committee and the California Debt Limit  

[6:3:66,270] Allocation Committee to issue an additional $500 million in state low-income housing tax credits in 2020 to be paired with 4%  

[6:4:66,280] federal low-income housing tax credits and tax-exempt bonds to bolster new construction of low-income housing. Per AB 101,  

[6:5:66,289] this $500 million tax credit was issued for calendar years beginning in 2020, and for calendar years beginning in 2021 pursuant  

[6:6:66,299] to an authorization in the annual Budget Act or related legislation.  

[6:7:66,316] Farmworker Housing Program  

[7:0:66,334] Chapter 521, Statutes of 2008, folded the previous Farmworker Housing Assistance Tax Credit program into the general state  

[7:1:66,344] LIHTC program as a $500,000 annual set-aside in support of farmworker housing. Pursuant to Chapter 372, Statutes of 2017,  

[7:2:66,353] these credits differ from other state credits in that the credit percentage for tax-exempt bond financed projects is higher and  

[7:3:66,363] projects may receive state credits even if they receive a federal tax credit basis increase.  

[7:4:66,380] Beginning in 2024 through 2034 calendar year, AB 1654 allocates $25,000,000 of the $500,000,000 state low-income housing  

[7:5:66,390] tax credits to be set aside for farmworker housing.  

[7:6:66,407] State Historic Rehabilitation Tax Credits  

[8:0:66,425] SB 451 authorized $50,000,000 in historic tax credits per calendar year, for a five-year period, beginning in 2021. These tax  

[8:1:66,434] credits are allocated by California Tax Credit Allocation Committee in conjunction with and upon approval from the Office of  

[8:2:66,444] Historic Preservation.

[6:0:88,199] 0971   California Alternative Energy and Advanced Transportation Financing  

[6:1:279,213] Authority  

[7:0:66,234] The California Alternative Energy and Advanced Transportation Financing Authority was established by Chapter 908, Statutes  

[7:1:66,244] of 1980, to promote the prompt and efficient development of energy sources which are renewable or which more efficiently  

[7:2:66,254] utilize and conserve scarce energy resources. Recognizing the importance of developing a secure energy future to protect the  

[7:3:66,264] environment and ensure economic stability, the Authority advances the state's goals of reducing greenhouse gas emissions by  

[7:4:66,273] providing financing options to assist industries in increasing the deployment of sustainable and renewable energy sources,  

[7:5:66,283] implementing measures that increase the efficiency of the use of energy, creating high quality employment opportunities, and  

[7:6:66,293] lessening the state's dependence on fossil fuels. To support these goals, the Authority provides financing and credit  

[7:7:66,303] enhancements to promote the establishment of facilities that use alternative methods and sources of energy, and facilities  

[7:8:66,312] needed for the development and commercialization of advanced transportation technologies.  

[8:0:66,332] The Authority consists of five members: the State Treasurer, who serves as the chairperson, the State Controller, the Director  

[8:1:66,342] of the Department of Finance, the Chairperson of the State Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission,  

[8:2:66,351] and the President of the Public Utilities Commission.

[5:0:66,189] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,208] 0850 - ALTERNATIVE ENERGY AND ADVANCED TRANSPORTATION FINANCING AUTHORITY  

[7:0:66,227] Financial assistance can take the form of loans, loan loss reserves, interest rate reductions, proceeds of bonds issued by the  

[7:1:66,237] Authority,   loan guarantees or other credit enhancements, contributions of money, or a combination thereof, as determined and  

[7:2:66,247] approved by resolution of the Authority.  

[8:0:66,264] The Authority is a conduit issuer of tax-exempt private activity bonds to finance local district heating or cooling facilities, and  

[8:1:66,274] private activity bonds for 501(c)(3) non-profits. Past issuances total more than $212 million in bond financing for green projects  

[8:2:66,284] in California.  

[9:0:66,303] In 2010, the Authority launched a sales and use tax exclusion (STE) program for advanced transportation and alternative  

[9:1:66,313] source manufacturing in California (Chapter 10, Statutes of 2010). The STE program   expanded   (Chapter 677, Statutes of  

[9:2:66,323] 2012)   to include advanced manufacturing, with an annual cap of $100 million available for STE awards. Chapter 768, Statutes  

[9:3:66,333] of 2015, further expanded the program to also include equipment primarily used to process recycled feedstock and equipment  

[9:4:66,342] that primarily utilizes recycled feedstock in the production of another product.   In 2019, Chapter 690, Statutes of 2019, extended  

[9:5:66,352] the STE program sunset date to January 1, 2026, and Chapter 672, Statutes of 2019, expanded the job creation-related criteria  

[9:6:66,362] by which CAEATFA must evaluate applications. In 2022, Chapter 251, Statutes of 2022, authorized CAEATFA an additional  

[9:7:66,372] $15 million per calendar year in STE for specific lithium projects for years 2022 to 2024. The objective of this program is to  

[9:8:66,381] promote the creation of California-based manufacturing, businesses, and jobs that are aimed at reducing greenhouse gases,  

[9:9:66,391] air and water pollution, and energy consumption.  

[10:0:66,411] Legislation in 2010 (Chapter 15, Statutes of 2010), 2011 (Chapter 9, Statutes of 2011, First Extraordinary Session), and 2013  

[10:1:66,420] (Chapter 356, Statutes of 2013), authorized financial assistance to encourage energy efficiency retrofits. The Authority  

[10:2:66,430] developed a loan loss reserve program   for residential whole house energy efficiency and renewable energy projects, and   a risk  

[10:3:66,440] mitigation program for residential Property Assessed Clean Energy programs in California. Programs to support low-interest  

[10:4:66,450] financing of Property Assessed Clean Energy efficiency projects in commercial buildings are in discussion.  

[11:0:66,469] The Budget Act of 2014 authorized administration of the California Hub for Energy Efficiency Financing on behalf of, and in  

[11:1:66,479] collaboration with, the California Public Utilities Commission   to develop and launch energy efficiency financing pilot programs   to  

[11:2:66,489] encourage and leverage private capital lending   (in both the residential and commercial sectors). These programs   employ  

[11:3:66,499] various types of credit enhancements, lower costs and expand access to capital for Californians making   energy efficiency  

[11:4:66,508] improvements   to help achieve the state's   energy efficiency and decarbonization   goals.

[3:0:151,99] 0974   California Pollution Control Financing Authority  

[4:0:66,120] California Pollution Control Financing Authority (CPCFA) is committed to stimulating local economies in California by making  

[4:1:66,130] capital more accessible to small businesses and delivering innovative financing for projects that protect and restore the  

[4:2:66,139] environment.  

[5:0:66,157] Created in 1972, CPCFA originated as a conduit issuer of tax-exempt, pollution-control bonds and now assists the solid-waste,  

[5:1:66,166] recycling, water, and wastewater industries through its Private Activity Tax-Exempt Bond Program. Over the last 50 years,  

[5:2:66,176] CPCFA evolved to include financing programs assisting California's small business sector through its California Capital Access  

[5:3:66,186] Program (CalCAP) and to promote the reuse and redevelopment of brownfields through the California Recycle Underutilized  

[5:4:66,196] Sites Program (CALReUSE).  

[6:0:66,215] CPCFA consists of the following members: State Treasurer (Chairperson), the State Controller, and the Director of the  

[6:1:66,225] Department of Finance.  

[7:0:66,255] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[0:0:66,13] 2023-24 STATE BUDGET — LJE   234 

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:116,32] 0974   California Pollution Control Financing Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] 0860 - POLLUTION CONTROL TAX-EXEMPT BOND PROGRAM  

[4:0:66,86] CPCFA’s Tax-Exempt Bond Program provides access to the tax-exempt bond market for private companies serving a public  

[4:1:66,96] purpose, through the use of private activity bonds. California businesses may use private activity bonds for the acquisition,  

[4:2:66,105] construction, or installation of qualified waste disposal, waste recovery, water furnishing, and wastewater treatment, or the  

[4:3:66,115] acquisition and installation of new equipment. In addition, CPCFA maintains a Small Business Assistance Fund (SBAF) that  

[4:4:66,125] provides cost of issuance financial assistance to qualified small business borrowers.  

[5:0:66,142] Tax-exempt financing issued by CPCFA assists municipalities in complying with waste diversion mandates of the California  

[5:1:66,152] Integrated Waste Management Act of 1989, the California Global Warming Solutions Act of 2006, and more recently, Assembly  

[5:2:66,162] Bill 1826 (Chesbro, Chapter 727, Statutes of 2014), and Senate Bill 1383 (Lara, Chapter 395, Statutes of 2016). As of June 30,  

[5:3:66,171] 2022, bonds totaling approximately $16.9 billion have been issued by CPCFA.  

[6:0:66,189] 0865 - CALIFORNIA CAPITAL ACCESS PROGRAM FOR SMALL BUSINESSES  

[7:0:66,206] CalCAP for Small Business incentivizes banks and other financial institutions to make loans to small businesses through the  

[7:1:66,216] use of a loan loss reserve account. The program works through the creation of a loan loss-reserve fund specific to each  

[7:2:66,226] participating financial institution. CPCFA, the financial institution, and the borrower all contribute to fund the reserve. CPCFA  

[7:3:66,235] provides additional incentives for lending to businesses located in underserved communities and disaster areas. The reserve  

[7:4:66,245] serves to reduce loan-loss risk, supporting financial institutions when they lend to California small businesses.  

[8:0:66,262] Prior to 2010, CalCAP was entirely self-funded with $38 million transferred from CPCFA’s Small Business Assistance Fund  

[8:1:66,272] (SBAF), established with fees collected from large companies utilizing CPCFA's tax-exempt bond program. In October 2010  

[8:2:66,282] CalCAP received $6 million from the General Fund (Chapter 731, Statutes of 2010) to increase lending efforts. The $6 million  

[8:3:66,292] appropriation was fully-expended by the end of 2018 allowing the annual recapture of contributions to become the primary  

[8:4:66,301] source for the State's contributions.  

[9:0:66,319] In 2011 California was awarded an allocation of federal funds in the amount of $168 million from U.S. Treasury's State Small  

[9:1:66,329] Business Credit Initiative (SSBCI). CPCFA shared the allocation with the Infrastructure and Economic Development Bank  

[9:2:66,338] (IBank), now housed within the Governor's Office of Business and Economic Development (GO-Biz).  

[10:0:66,356] These funds enabled CPCFA to expand the availability of its CalCAP for Small Business (CalCAP SB). In addition, CPCFA  

[10:1:66,365] launched the Collateral Support Program (CSP) to further offset financial institution’s resistance to lend after the 2008  

[10:2:66,375] recession. CPCFA created the CSP with a recapture provision allowing for the sustainability of the program.  

[11:0:66,392] The SSBCI program ended in 2017. In an effort to sustain the program past the sunset date, CalCAP SB instituted a recapture  

[11:1:66,402] provision. Funds are being recaptured annually to sustain the CalCAP SB and the CSP.   In 2021, the federal government  

[11:2:66,412] reauthorized the funding of SSBCI (SSBCI 2.0). CPCFA and IBank partnered again to apply for SSBCI 2.0 allocation and will  

[11:3:66,422] share the $1.181 billion allocated to California. Recapture has been suspended for the SSBCI 2.0 program.  

[12:0:66,439] Utilizing the CalCAP model, CPCFA partnered with the California Air Resources Board (CARB) to assist truck and bus owners  

[12:1:66,449] in meeting new clean air requirements by directing a total of $215.9 million in Air Quality Improvement Program funds to  

[12:2:66,459] incentivize lending for cleaner vehicles that comply with CARB's Statewide Truck and Bus Rule. CPCFA also partnered with the  

[12:3:66,468] California Energy Commission (CEC) to implement a $2 million pilot program to provide credit enhancements and rebates to  

[12:4:66,478] small business borrowers and multi-unit dwellings to install electric vehicle charging stations. The CalCAP Electric Vehicle  

[12:5:66,488] Charging Station (EVCS) Financing Program ended March 31, 2022.  

[13:0:66,505] 0870 - CALIFORNIA RECYCLE UNDERUTILIZED SITES PROGRAM  

[14:0:66,522] The California Recycle Underutilized Sites (CALReUSE) program assists with the reuse and redevelopment of underutilized  

[14:1:66,532] properties with real or perceived contamination issues. CALReUSE addresses a funding and information gap in the  

[14:2:66,542] development of contaminated properties (brownfields) to help bring these properties into productive reuse. Beginning in 2000,  

[14:3:66,552] the CALReUSE Assessment Program provided forgivable loans of up to $300,000 which was later increased to $500,000 per  

[14:4:66,561] brownfield site. The funds assisted with brownfield site assessment and characterization, technical assistance, and the  

[14:5:66,571] development of remedial action plans. Eligible projects included sites with potential beneficial reuse not currently redeveloped  

[14:6:66,581] due to lack of information about real or perceived contamination, uncertainty about clean-up costs, or concerns regarding time  

[14:7:66,591] frames and the regulatory process.  

[15:0:66,608] Additionally, Proposition 1C, the Housing and Emergency Shelter Trust Fund Act of 2006, included provisions to fund  

[15:1:66,618] brownfield cleanup that resulted in the expansion of the CALReUSE Program. The CALReUSE Remediation Program received  

[15:2:66,628] $60 million in Proposition 1C funds  

[16:0:66,645] and provided loans and grants up to $5 million per project for brownfield remediation that promoted affordable infill residential  

[16:1:66,655] and mixed-used developments. Three projects were pre-approved to receive awards of more than $5 million; one grant was  

[16:2:66,664] increased above $5 million when returned funds became available. All CALReUSE Program funds have been awarded. The  

[16:3:66,674] CALReUSE Program's $60 million investment in cleanup of contaminated sites in 30 California communities has leveraged the

[3:10:66,181] loans.   The program also offers borrowers a rebate toward the cost of the Certified Access Specialist (CAS) Report.

[4:2:66,238] and residential property owners (including multi-unit dwellings and mobile homes registered by the California Department of  

[4:3:66,247] Housing and Community Development) for costs related to seismic retrofitting. The Program encourages banks and other  

[4:4:66,257] financial institutions to participate by creating loan loss reserve accounts for lenders that enroll qualifying loans into the

[10:0:154,410] 0977   California Health Facilities Financing Authority  

[11:0:66,431] The mission of the California Health Facilities Financing Authority is to help eligible and credit worthy nonprofit and public  

[11:1:66,441] health facilities reduce their cost of capital, and promote health care improvement and cost containment objectives by providing  

[11:2:66,451] cost-effective tax-exempt bond, low-cost loan, and direct grant programs.  

[12:0:66,468] The Authority was established by Chapter 1033, Statutes of 1979 (AB 1558), and consists of nine members: the State  

[12:1:66,478] Treasurer, the Director of the Department of Finance, the State Controller, two public members appointed by the Senate, two  

[12:2:66,487] public members appointed by the Assembly, and two public members appointed by the Governor.

[11:0:66,545] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[11:1:66,564] 0880 - CHILDREN'S HOSPITAL PROGRAM  

[12:0:66,581] In 2004, California voters approved Proposition 61, which established the Children's Hospital Program. In 2008, Proposition 3  

[12:1:66,591] established the second Children's Hospital Program. In 2018, Proposition 4 established a third Children’s Hospital Program.  

[12:2:66,601] The purpose of the three programs is to improve the health and welfare of California's critically ill children by providing a stable  

[12:3:66,610] and ready source of funds for capital improvement projects for children's hospitals and, under Proposition 4, hospitals that  

[12:4:66,620] provide pediatric services for children eligible for California Children’s Services. Children's Hospital Program grant funding is  

[12:5:66,630] derived from the issuance of general obligation bonds, up to $750 million under Proposition 61, $980 million under Proposition  

[12:6:66,640] 3, and $1.5 billion under Proposition 4.  

[12:7:66,657] 0885 - HEALTH FACILITIES BONDS, LOANS, AND GRANTS  

[13:0:66,677] The Authority serves as a conduit provider of tax-exempt bonds and a direct provider of financial assistance to health  

[13:1:66,686] institutions.  

[14:0:66,706] Tax-Exempt Bond Program  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 241 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:410,13] 2023-24 STATE BUDGET — LJE   239 

[2:0:119,32] 0977   California Health Facilities Financing Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] Bonds issued under this conduit program are not a debt, liability, or a pledge of the full faith and credit of the taxing power of  

[3:1:66,78] the state or any of its political subdivisions. The full faith and credit of the participating institutions are pledged for repayment of  

[3:2:66,88] the bonds. To qualify for funding, the proposed project must be a health facility, operated by a private nonprofit corporation or  

[3:3:66,98] association, city, city and county, county, or hospital district.  

[4:0:66,117] HELP II Loan Program  

[5:0:66,135] The Authority administers the Healthcare Expansion Loan Program II (HELP II) to assist small and rural health facilities in  

[5:1:66,144] obtaining financing for their capital needs. The HELP II Program provides two percent and three percent fixed interest rate  

[5:2:66,154] loans of up to $2 million to California's nonprofit small and rural health facilities in an efficient, timely, and cost-effective manner.  

[5:3:66,164] HELP II loans may be used to purchase or construct new facilities, remodel or renovate existing facilities, purchase equipment  

[5:4:66,174] and/or furnishings, and refinance existing debt.  

[6:0:66,191] Distressed Hospital Loan Program  

[7:0:66,208] Chapter 6, Statutes of 2023 (AB 112) and AB 118 establish the Distressed Hospital Loan Program administered by the  

[7:1:66,218] Department of Health Care Access and Information, in collaboration with the Authority, to provide up to $300 million in interest-

[7:2:66,228] free, cashflow loans to not-for-profit hospitals and public hospitals in significant financial distress, and certain closed hospitals.  

[8:0:66,245] Nondesignated Public Hospital Bridge Loan Program  

[9:0:66,262] The Nondesignated Public Hospital Bridge Loan Program provides cashflow loans to eligible nondesignated public hospitals  

[9:1:66,272] affected by the financial impacts of the COVID-19 public health emergency. These loans are required to be paid back within  

[9:2:66,282] two years and are secured by the borrower’s Medi-Cal reimbursements. The 2021 Budget Act included $40 million one-time  

[9:3:66,292] General Fund and the 2022 Budget Act included $40 million one-time General Fund to support this program.  

[10:0:66,309] Specialty Dental Clinic Grant Program  

[11:0:66,326] The Specialty Dental Clinic Grant Program provides competitive grants to eligible entities to support the construction,  

[11:1:66,336] expansion, modification, or adaptation of specialty dental clinics to increase access to oral health care for the special health  

[11:2:66,346] care needs populations. The 2022 Budget Act included $25 million one-time General Fund and the 2023 Budget Act includes  

[11:3:66,356] $25 million one-time General Fund to support this program.  

[12:0:66,373] 0890 - MENTAL HEALTH WELLNESS GRANTS  

[13:0:66,390] • Investment in Mental Health Wellness Act of 2013 Grant Program 

[14:0:66,407] Chapter 34, Statutes of 2013 (SB 82), the Investment in Mental Health Wellness Act of 2013 established a grant program to  

[14:1:66,417] disburse funds to California counties or to their nonprofit or public agency designees for the purpose of developing mental  

[14:2:66,427] health crisis support programs. Specifically, funds may be used to increase capacity for client assistance and services in crisis  

[14:3:66,437] intervention, crisis stabilization, crisis residential treatment, rehabilitative mental health services, and mobile crisis support  

[14:4:66,446] teams. The grants will support capital improvement, expansion and limited start-up costs.  

[15:0:66,466] • Investment in Mental Health Wellness Grant Program for Children and Youth  

[16:0:66,483] Chapter 30, Statutes of 2016 (SB 833), Section 20 established the Investment in Mental Health Wellness Grant Program for  

[16:1:66,493] Children and Youth to increase mental health services for children and youth 21 years of age and under to develop a complete  

[16:2:66,503] continuum of crisis services. Working with counties, the program funds facility acquisition, construction/renovation, equipment  

[16:3:66,513] acquisition, applicable startup or expansion costs, and limited personnel funding to develop crisis residential treatment and  

[16:4:66,522] crisis stabilization facilities, mobile crisis support teams, and family respite care facilities.  

[17:0:66,542] • Community Services Infrastructure Grants 

[18:0:66,559] Chapter 33, Statutes of 2016 (SB 843), Section 52 established the Community Services Infrastructure Grant Program to  

[18:1:66,569] expand community alternatives to jail and prison. The program seeks to expand access to jail and prison diversion programs  

[18:2:66,579] and services for those with mental health illness, substance use disorders, or who have suffered from trauma. Working with  

[18:3:66,589] counties, the program funds facility acquisition, renovation, equipment acquisition, and applicable startup or expansion costs for  

[18:4:66,598] facilities that provide services to this population.  

[19:0:66,616] • No Place Like Home Program  

[20:0:66,633] Chapter 43, Statutes of 2016 (AB 1618) established the No Place Like Home (NPLH) Program, which requires the Department  

[20:1:66,643] of Housing and Community Development (HCD) to award up to $2 billion to counties for the acquisition, design, construction,  

[20:2:66,652] rehabilitation, or preservation of permanent supportive housing for individuals living with a severe mental illness who are  

[20:3:66,662] homeless or at risk of chronic homelessness. Chapter 322, Statutes of 2016 (AB 1628) authorized CHFFA to enter into  

[20:4:66,672] contracts with HCD and further authorized CHFFA to issue up to $2 billion in taxable revenue bonds pursuant to the NPLH  

[20:5:66,682] Program. Chapter 41, Statutes of 2018 (AB 1827) placed the NPLH Program on the November 2018 ballot (Proposition 2),  

[20:6:66,692] where it was adopted by the voters as the No Place Like Home Act. This ratified existing law establishing the NPLH Program  

[20:7:66,701] as being consistent with the Mental Health Services Act approved through Proposition 63 in 2004. It also ratified the issuance  

[20:8:66,711] of up to $2 billion in previously authorized bonds.  

[21:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[13:0:66,672] The California Achieving a Better Life Experience Act (ABLE) Board was established by Chapter 796, Statutes of 2015 (SB  

[13:1:66,682] 324) to create a Qualified ABLE Program. The Program will provide eligible individuals with disabilities the opportunity to save  

[13:2:66,691] private funds in tax-advantaged ABLE accounts for the purpose of supporting persons with disabilities to maintain their health,  

[13:3:66,701] independence, and quality of life.

[11:0:66,494] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[11:1:66,513] 0895 - CALIFORNIA ABLE ACT BOARD  

[12:0:66,532] On December 19, 2014, President Obama signed the Stephen Beck Jr., Achieving a Better Life Experience Act of 2014, which  

[12:1:66,542] allows individuals who became blind or disabled before reaching age 26, to create tax-free savings accounts. Individuals can  

[12:2:66,552] make nondeductible cash contributions to an ABLE account in the name of a designated beneficiary with tax-free earnings.  

[12:3:66,562] ABLE account distributions are also not included in the beneficiary's income, as long as they are used for qualified disability  

[12:4:66,572] expenses. Chapter 774, Statutes of 2015 (AB 449), implements the ABLE Act in California, and directs the Board, with the  

[12:5:66,581] State Treasurer as Chair, to administer the California ABLE Program accounts on behalf of eligible individuals.  

[13:0:66,601] The Board and the California ABLE Program Trust were established by Chapter 796, Statutes of 2015 (SB 324), for the  

[13:1:66,611] purpose of creating the Program. CalABLE was officially launched in December of 2018 with the mission to meet the diverse  

[13:2:66,620] needs of our customers and their families. CalABLE’s pledge is to be customer-driven, accountable, and a trusted partner in  

[13:3:66,630] providing financial services.  

[14:0:66,647] Under the Program, a person may make contributions of up to $17,000 during a taxable year, to a designated beneficiary's  

[14:1:66,657] ABLE account to be used for the qualified disability expenses of that designated beneficiary. Funds may accumulate and grow  

[14:2:66,667] in CalABLE investments with no federal or state income taxes or penalties as long as the money is used to pay for qualified  

[14:3:66,677] expenses when withdrawn. 

[15:0:66,694] In 2022, Governor Newsom signed AB 2216 into law, limiting state protections from Medi-Cal recovery upon the death of an  

[15:1:66,704] ABLE beneficiary, only to CalABLE account holders, and permitting CalABLE account holders who are working to contribute up  

[15:2:66,714] to the full amount allowable under the federal ABLE to Work provision.  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[5:0:181,140] 0984   CalSavers Retirement Savings Board  

[5:1:66,161] The   CalSavers Retirement Savings Board (Board) was established by Chapter 734, Statutes of 2012 (SB 1234). Chapter 804,  

[5:2:66,171] Statutes of 2016 (SB 1234), subsequently authorized the Board to implement the CalSavers Retirement Savings Program  

[5:3:66,180] (CalSavers), a state-administered retirement savings program for private sector employees in California with no access to  

[5:4:66,190] workplace retirement savings plans. The Board’s mission is to ensure all Californians have a path to financial security in  

[5:5:66,200] retirement by providing a simple, portable, low-cost way for workers to invest in their futures.  

[6:0:66,217] The Board consists of nine members: the State Treasurer (Chairperson), the Director of the Department of Finance, the State  

[6:1:66,227] Controller, a retirement savings and investment expert appointed by the Senate Committee on Rules, an employee  

[6:2:66,237] representative appointed by the Speaker of the Assembly, a small business representative appointed by the Governor, a public  

[6:3:66,247] member appointed by the Governor, and two additional members appointed by the Governor.

[6:0:185,222] 0985   California School Finance Authority  

[7:0:66,243] The California School Finance Authority (CSFA), created in 1985, provides facilities and working capital financing to school  

[7:1:66,253] districts, community college districts, county offices of education, and charter schools. The State Treasurer serves as chair of  

[7:2:66,262] CSFA, and the Superintendent of Public Instruction and the Director of Finance serve as members. CSFA has offices in Los  

[7:3:66,272] Angeles and Sacramento.  

[8:0:66,290] CSFA serves as the primary state agency for California charter schools seeking facility and working capital funding through  

[8:1:66,299] several state and federally funded programs.

[7:0:66,354] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:1:66,373] 0918 - SMART BONDS PROGRAM  

[8:0:66,392] Chapter 1438, Statutes of 1985, as amended by Chapter 598, Statutes of 1987, established CSFA and authorized the issuance  

[8:1:66,402] of revenue bonds or other debt instruments. The proceeds from the sale of the bonds are available for loans to schools and  

[8:2:66,412] community college districts to assist with the acquisition of equipment and new school sites, construction of new facilities,  

[8:3:66,422] reconstruction of existing facilities, capital improvements, and to provide working capital. Chapter 325, Statutes of 2006 (AB  

[8:4:66,431] 2717), allows CSFA to issue debt on behalf of California charter schools for capital improvements and working capital needs.  

[8:5:66,449] 0920 - CHARTER SCHOOL FACILITIES PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,468] Chapter 935, Statutes of 2002, as amended by Chapter 587, Statutes of 2003, created the Charter School Facilities Program  

[9:1:66,478] (CSFP), which provides funding for the new construction, renovation or rehabilitation of charter school facilities. CSFA and the  

[9:2:66,488] Office of Public School Construction jointly administer the program. CSFP was funded through the Kindergarten-University  

[9:3:66,497] Public Education Facilities Bond Acts of 2002, 2004, 2006, and 2016, for a total of $1.4 billion. The program provides a 50  

[9:4:66,507] percent state subsidy for charter school facilities project costs, with the balance of the project costs being repaid (to the state)  

[9:5:66,517] by the charter school via a 30-year loan. Recipient charter schools must provide site-based instruction, be financially sound,  

[9:6:66,527] and meet other program eligibility requirements. 

[10:0:66,544] 0925 - STATE CHARTER SCHOOL FACILITIES INCENTIVE GRANTS PROGRAM  

[11:0:66,561] In 2004, 2009, and 2014, CSFA received grants under the United States Department of Education’s State Charter School  

[11:1:66,571] Facilities Incentive Grants Program (CFDA 84.282D) to provide California charter schools with assistance for facilities costs.  

[11:2:66,581] Grant funds reimburse a charter school’s costs for rent, lease, mortgage or debt service payments for existing or new facilities  

[11:3:66,591] or the costs of acquiring land and/or constructing or renovating a facility. Grants are awarded to charter schools based on  

[11:4:66,600] preference points assigned based on: the percentage of low-income students; percentage of overcrowding; not-for-profit status;  

[11:5:66,610] and demonstrated student performance. Schools cannot use program funds to supplant state funding. This program sunset in  

[11:6:66,620] December 2022.  

[12:0:66,637] 0930 - CHARTER SCHOOL FACILITY GRANT PROGRAM  

[13:0:66,655] The Charter School Facility Grant Program provides Proposition 98 General Fund grants to assist charter schools with rent and  

[13:1:66,664] lease costs. This program targets schools and communities with significant populations of economically disadvantaged  

[13:2:66,674] students. Eligible charter schools are provided the lesser of $1,421 per unit of eligible average daily attendance, or 75 percent  

[13:3:66,684] of annual facilities rent and lease costs.  

[13:4:66,701] 0935 - CHARTER SCHOOL REVOLVING LOAN FUND  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:3:66,98] not opened nor completed their full charter term.

[5:0:168,156] 0989   California Educational Facilities Authority  

[5:1:66,177] The California Educational Facilities Authority (CEFA) provides assistance to qualified nonprofit higher education institutions  

[5:2:66,187] through a tax-exempt revenue bond program to reduce the costs of financing academic facilities.  

[6:0:66,204] CEFA consists of the following five members: the Director of Finance, the State Controller, the State Treasurer, and two public  

[6:1:66,214] members appointed by the Governor.

[15:0:66,654] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[15:1:66,673] 0940 - BOND FINANCING  

[16:0:66,690] CEFA issues tax-exempt revenue bonds to assist private educational institutions of higher learning to construct educational  

[16:1:66,700] facilities. Because it is authorized to issue tax-exempt bonds, CEFA may be able to provide more favorable financing terms  

[16:2:66,710] than might otherwise be obtainable. CEFA also may include qualifying nonprofit entities as eligible program participants for the  

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 256 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] 2023-24 STATE BUDGET — LJE   254 

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:133,32] 0989   California Educational Facilities Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] construction of student and faculty housing. Bonds issued by CEFA are not a debt, liability, or claim on the faith and credit or  

[3:1:66,78] the taxing power of the State of California or any of its political subdivisions. The full faith and credit of the participating  

[3:2:66,88] institution is pledged to the payment of the bonds. Bonds issued for this purpose are not subject to the state's "private activity"  

[3:3:66,98] bond ceiling as specified in federal law.  

[4:0:66,127] For purposes of the California Educational Facilities Act, "private college" or "participating private college" means a nonprofit,  

[4:1:66,137] private, regionally accredited, degree-granting college that does not restrict the admission of a student based on the student's  

[4:2:66,146] race or ethnicity and provided that the financing does not violate constitutional provisions.  

[5:0:66,176] "Bond" means bonds, notes, debentures, securities, or other evidences of indebtedness. This broad definition allows CEFA to  

[5:1:66,185] issue tax-exempt public offering or private placement loans for participating institutions, which may provide greater cost savings  

[5:2:66,195] than these colleges and universities might otherwise realize on a traditional bond financing.  

[6:0:66,215] 0955 - COLLEGE ACCESS TAX CREDIT FUND  

[7:0:66,232] Chapter 367, Statutes of 2014, required CEFA to administer the College Access Tax Credit Fund and allocate and certify the  

[7:1:66,242] tax credits for taxable years beginning on or after January 1, 2014, and before January 1, 2017. Chapter 22, Statutes of 2015,  

[7:2:66,251] added a requirement that CEFA continue to allocate and certify the tax credits for taxable years beginning on or after January  

[7:3:66,261] 1, 2017, and before January 1, 2018. Chapter 527, Statutes of 2017, extended CEFA's responsibility to administer the tax  

[7:4:66,271] credits for taxable years beginning on or after January 1, 2017, and before January 1, 2023.   Chapter 976, Statues of 2022,  

[7:5:66,281] extended CEFA’s responsibility further to taxable years beginning before January 1, 2028.
